reference PyTorch source (the prompt the agent sees)
import torch
import torch.nn as nn


class Model(nn.Module):
    """
    Channel-wise gated delta attention recurrence.

    The recurrent state decays independently for each output channel, which
    makes the update closer to channel-gated delta attention variants used in
    fast long-context baselines.
    """

    def __init__(self):
        super().__init__()

    def forward(
        self,
        q: torch.Tensor,
        k: torch.Tensor,
        v: torch.Tensor,
        gate: torch.Tensor,
    ) -> torch.Tensor:
        batch_size, seq_len, num_heads, head_dim = q.shape
        state = torch.zeros(batch_size, num_heads, head_dim, head_dim, dtype=q.dtype, device=q.device)
        outputs = []

        for t in range(seq_len):
            gate_t = gate[:, t].unsqueeze(-2)
            kv_outer = k[:, t].unsqueeze(-1) * v[:, t].unsqueeze(-2)
            state = gate_t * state + kv_outer
            y_t = (q[:, t].unsqueeze(-1) * state).sum(dim=-2)
            outputs.append(y_t)

        return torch.stack(outputs, dim=1)


batch_size = 2
seq_len = 128
num_heads = 4
head_dim = 24


def get_inputs():
    q = torch.randn(batch_size, seq_len, num_heads, head_dim, dtype=torch.float32)
    k = torch.randn(batch_size, seq_len, num_heads, head_dim, dtype=torch.float32)
    v = torch.randn(batch_size, seq_len, num_heads, head_dim, dtype=torch.float32)
    gate = torch.sigmoid(torch.randn(batch_size, seq_len, num_heads, head_dim, dtype=torch.float32))
    return [q, k, v, gate]


def get_init_inputs():
    return []

We need to write a custom CUDA kernel that computes channel-wise gated delta attention recurrence, matching the PyTorch reference. The reference computes, for each time step t: - gate_t = gate[:, t].unsqueeze(-2) -> shape (B, num_heads, 1, head_dim) - kv_outer = k[:, t].unsqueeze(-1) * v[:, t].unsqueeze(-2) -> shape (B, num_heads, head_dim, head_dim) - state = gate_t * state + kv_outer - y_t = (q[:, t].unsqueeze(-1) * state).sum(dim=-2) -> shape (B, num_heads, head_dim) Here, q,k,v have shape (B, seq_len, num_heads, head_dim). gate has shape (B, seq_len, num_heads, head_dim). state is (B, num_heads, head_dim, head_dim). gate_t is broadcasted over the first dimension of state? Wait: state shape is (B, num_heads, head_dim, head_dim). gate_t shape is (B, num_heads, 1, head_dim). So gate_t * state broadcasts: the last dimension of gate_t (head_dim) aligns with last dimension of state (head_dim). The 1 aligns with the third dimension (head_dim). So gate_t scales each row (the third dimension) of state differently per head and per channel? Actually, it's per output channel? Wait state dimensions: (B, H, head_dim, head_dim). The third dim is row? Let's see: kv_outer = k[:,t].unsqueeze(-1) * v[:,t].unsqueeze(-2). k[:,t] is (B,H,head_dim,1). v[:,t] is (B,H,1,head_dim). So kv_outer[i,j] = k_i * v_j. So the first head_dim index corresponds to k dimension, second to v dimension? Then gate_t shape is (B,H,1,head_dim). Multiplying gate_t * state scales each column (the last dim) of state? Actually broadcasting: gate_t has dims (B,H,1,head_dim). state has (B,H,head_dim,head_dim). So gate_t is broadcast over the third dimension (size head_dim), meaning each column j is scaled by gate_t[..., j]. So the state update decays each column independently. Then y_t = (q[:, t].unsqueeze(-1) * state).sum(dim=-2). q[:,t] unsqueezed is (B,H,head_dim,1). Multiply with state (B,H,head_dim,head_dim) broadcasts over last dim, so each row i is scaled by q_i. Summing over dim=-2 (the third dim) gives sum over i of q_i * state_{i,j}. So output y_t[j] = sum_i q_i * state_{i,j}. Which is like q^T @ state (where state is head_dim x head_dim matrix, q is vector, v is vector). But kv_outer is outer product of k and v. So state = gate * state + k_i^T v_j? Wait k is row vector, v is row vector. Outer product is k_i * v_j. Then state accumulates outer products weighted by gate. Then y = q^T @ state (sum_i q_i * state_{i,j}). So overall, this is like a linear attention with gate decay per channel (column). This is similar to an RNN where state is a matrix and gate is applied element-wise per column. We need to write a CUDA kernel that does this efficiently, fusing the loop over time steps. Since it's a recurrence, we cannot easily parallelize over t within a single sequence; but we can parallelize over batch, heads, and maybe channels. However, the reference does the naive loop in Python, which is very slow because each iteration launches many small kernels. Implementation details for CUDA kernel: ``` template <typename scalar_t> __global__ void gated_delta_attention_kernel( const scalar_t* __restrict__ q, const scalar_t* __restrict__ k, const scalar_t* __restrict__ v, const scalar_t* __restrict__ gate, scalar_t* __restrict__ out, int batch_size, int seq_len, int num_heads, int head_dim) { int b = blockIdx.x / num_heads; int h = blockIdx.x % num_heads; int i = threadIdx.x; // 0..31 (actually < head_dim) int j = threadIdx.y; // 0..head_dim-1 // Only active if i < head_dim bool active = (i < head_dim); // strides int stride_b = seq_len * num_heads * head_dim; int stride_t = num_heads * head_dim; int stride_h = head_dim; int base = b * stride_b + h * stride_h; // State register scalar_t state = 0.0f; // Shared memory for q,k,v,gate extern __shared__ char smem[]; scalar_t* sq = (scalar_t*)smem; // head_dim scalar_t* sk = sq + head_dim; // head_dim scalar_t* sv = sk + head_dim; // head_dim scalar_t* sg = sv + head_dim; // head_dim for (int t = 0; t < seq_len; ++t) { int t_offset = t * stride_t; // Load into shared memory // Use threads with x < head_dim, y == 0 for q and k if (i < head_dim && j == 0) { sq[i] = q[base + t_offset + i]; sk[i] = k[base + t_offset + i]; } // Use threads with x == 0, y < head_dim for v and gate if (i == 0 && j < head_dim) { sv[j] = v[base + t_offset + j]; sg[j] = gate[base + t_offset + j]; } __syncthreads(); if (active) { scalar_t kv = sk[i] * sv[j]; state = sg[j] * state + kv; scalar_t contrib = sq[i] * state; // Warp reduction over x dimension (i) for fixed j // Use warp shuffle #pragma unroll for (int offset = 16; offset > 0; offset /= 2) { contrib += __shfl_down_sync(0xffffffff, contrib, offset); } // Actually, only need to reduce over i=0..head_dim-1. Since warp size is 32, // and head_dim <= 32, a single shuffle reduction over 32 is fine. if (i == 0) { out[base + t_offset + j] = contrib; } } __syncthreads(); } } ``` Wait, the reduction: if head_dim=24, we reduce over 32 threads. The extra threads (i>=24) have contrib = ??? They are inactive. We should set their contrib to 0.0f to not affect the sum. So we should ensure active is false => contrib = 0.0f. But there's a subtlety: __shfl_down_sync reduces across all 32 threads in the warp. If some threads are inactive (i>=24) and have garbage in contrib, we need to zero them out. So set `scalar_t contrib = active ? sq[i] * state : 0.0f;`. Then reduce. Another subtlety: the warp reduction uses full mask 0xffffffff. Since inactive threads still participate in the shuffle. That's okay if their contrib is zero. After reduction, thread i=0 writes the output. But what about the case where j varies? Since each warp corresponds to one j (because blockDim.x = 32, blockDim.y = 24, so threads with same threadIdx.y are in the same warp? Actually, in CUDA, threads are grouped into warps in x-dimension first. So for blockDim=(32,24), the linear thread ID is threadIdx.x + threadIdx.y * blockDim.x. So threads with threadIdx.y = j have linear IDs from j*32 to j*32+31. Thus they form a warp (since 32 consecutive linear IDs). Good. So each warp is one column j. The first thread of the warp (i=0) writes the result for column j. Shared memory size: 4 * head_dim * sizeof(scalar_t). For head_dim=24, that's 384 bytes. We need to allocate `4 * head_dim * sizeof(scalar_t)` bytes. But wait: `stride_b = seq_len * num_heads * head_dim`. Actually the tensor shape is (batch_size, seq_len, num_heads, head_dim). So stride for batch = seq_len * num_heads * head_dim. Then for time t, offset = t * num_heads * head_dim. Then for head h, offset = h * head_dim. So total index = b * batch_stride + t * t_stride + h * h_stride + i. Yes. We need to make sure the output layout matches: output shape is (batch_size, seq_len, num_heads, head_dim). The reference returns `torch.stack(outputs, dim=1)`. `outputs` is a list of tensors of shape (batch_size, num_heads, head_dim). Stacking on dim=1 gives (batch_size, seq_len, num_heads, head_dim). So output layout is same as input. Thus `out[base + t_offset + j]` is correct, where base = b * seq_len * num_heads * head_dim + h * head_dim. Wait, we need to include t_offset inside the loop. Let's define: ``` int batch_stride = seq_len * num_heads * head_dim; int t_stride = num_heads * head_dim; int h_stride = head_dim; int idx = b * batch_stride + t * t_stride + h * h_stride + i; ``` Yes. Let's write this kernel in detail: ``` template <typename scalar_t> __global__ void gated_delta_attention_kernel( const scalar_t* __restrict__ q, const scalar_t* __restrict__ k, const scalar_t* __restrict__ v, const scalar_t* __restrict__ gate, scalar_t* __restrict__ out, int batch_size, int seq_len, int num_heads, int head_dim) { // Each block handles one column j of one head h of one batch b int total_blocks_per_sample = num_heads * head_dim; int b = blockIdx.x / total_blocks_per_sample; int rem = blockIdx.x % total_blocks_per_sample; int h = rem / head_dim; int j = rem % head_dim; int tid = threadIdx.x; int lane = tid % 32; // not needed int batch_stride = seq_len * num_heads * head_dim; int t_stride = num_heads * head_dim; int h_stride = head_dim; int base = b * batch_stride + h * h_stride; // Shared memory layout: state[head_dim], v, gate extern __shared__ char smem[]; scalar_t* state = (scalar_t*)smem; scalar_t* sv_ptr = (scalar_t*)(smem + head_dim * sizeof(scalar_t)); scalar_t* sg_ptr = sv_ptr + 1; // Initialize state to zero // We can have each thread zero its portion for (int ii = tid; ii < head_dim; ii += blockDim.x) { state[ii] = 0.0f; } __syncthreads(); for (int t = 0; t < seq_len; ++t) { int t_offset = t * t_stride; // Load v_j and gate_j if (tid == 0) { *sv_ptr = v[base + t_offset + j]; *sg_ptr = gate[base + t_offset + j]; } __syncthreads(); scalar_t v_j_val = *sv_ptr; scalar_t g_j_val = *sg_ptr; scalar_t partial = 0.0f; for (int ii = tid; ii < head_dim; ii += blockDim.x) { scalar_t q_i = q[base + t_offset + ii]; scalar_t k_i = k[base + t_offset + ii]; scalar_t s = state[ii]; s = g_j_val * s + k_i * v_j_val; state[ii] = s; partial += q_i * s; } // Warp reduce partial sum across 32 threads #pragma unroll for (int offset = 16; offset > 0; offset /= 2) { partial += __shfl_down_sync(0xffffffff, partial, offset); } if (tid == 0) { out[base + t_offset + j] = partial; } __syncthreads(); // ensure all threads done before next iteration? Actually not needed because state is fully written and next iteration reads after sync at top. But we need a sync before next __syncthreads at load? The loop starts with __syncthreads at load of v/g. But we need to make sure all threads have finished writing state before any thread reads v/g for next t? Since v/g load writes to shared memory and state is separate shared memory, no conflict. However, the __syncthreads at top of next loop iteration ensures all threads are ready. But there is a potential issue: __syncthreads inside loop at the end may be needed to prevent warp race? Actually, after partial reduction, thread 0 writes output. Then we go to next t. At the start of next t, we do `if (tid == 0) { *sv_ptr = ...; } __syncthreads();`. This sync ensures all threads see the new v_j and g_j. The state writes from previous iteration are done by all threads before they reach the sync (since they all participate). The kv_outer only depends on i and j as k_i * v_j. That's rank-1 update. So for each (b,h,j), the recurrence for the vector s_i = state[:, :, i, j] (vector length D) is: s_i^{new} = gate_j * s_i^{old} + k_i * v_j And output_j = sum_i q_i * s_i^{new} This is exactly what my kernel does: one block per (b, h, j). Shared memory holds s_i (size D). The gate and v_j are loaded into shared memory. Then for each timestep, each thread loops over i, updates s_i, accumulates q_i * s_i into partial sum. reasoning
The issue with compiling is that the shared object is finding the system libstdc++ first, which does not have the CXXABI_1.3.15 symbol required by the pybind11 headers that PyTorch uses. We need to ensure that the linker embeds an RPATH that points to the PyTorch `lib` directory so that the loader picks up the newer libstdc++ bundled with PyTorch rather than the system one. But passing `extra_ldflags` to `load_inline` should have worked, unless the order in which the link arguments are placed is wrong. Let's try setting the environment variable and the LD_LIBRARY_PATH. Also, another approach is to wrap the load_inline in a try-except and use os.environ to set LD_LIBRARY_PATH or use `LD_PRELOAD`. But that might not help for the import of the compiled module itself because the DSO is already loaded with the wrong library path. Actually, the issue is that `torch.utils.cpp_extension.load_inline` uses `torch.utils.cpp_extension.load` which compiles into a temporary SO file, and then imports it. Python's `importlib.util.module_from_spec` loads the .so file, and the dynamic linker looks at `LD_LIBRARY_PATH` to resolve libraries. If we can set `LD_LIBRARY_PATH` before `load_inline`, then it should work. But `LD_LIBRARY_PATH` is checked only at process startup; modifying it during runtime via `os.environ` might not affect `dlopen()` in Python. However, `torch.utils.cpp_extension.load` eventually calls `dlopen()` via `importlib`, and `dlopen` does look at the process environment variable `LD_LIBRARY_PATH`, but actually, the caching behavior of the dynamic linker can be tricky. According to the man page for `dlopen` on Linux, `LD_LIBRARY_PATH` is searched after the `rpath`/`runpath`. The system has a pre-cached search list, so modifying `LD_LIBRARY_PATH` after the process has started doesn't always work. But we should try to set it before loading anyway. But the compilation already happened in the tool call, likely within the same Python process. So setting `os.environ['LD_LIBRARY_PATH']` before our code might not help. Another way is to inject `RPATH` properly. When `load_inline` compiles, it calls `torch.utils.cpp_extension.load` -> `torch.utils.cpp_extension._jit_compile` -> `torch.utils.cpp_extension._write_ninja_file_and_build_library`. We can write a helper function that creates a `.cu` file, calls `nvcc` to compile it to a shared library, and then opens it with `ctypes`. The C API would be simple: `void gated_delta_attention_forward(const float* q, const float* k, const float* v, const float* gate, float* out, int batch_size, int seq_len, int num_heads, int head_dim)`. Then in `ModelNew.forward`, we can get the data pointers with `.data_ptr()` and call the ctypes function. This avoids any C++ standard library dependency issues because we won't include `<torch/extension.h>` or use pybind11. The kernel can just use raw pointers. Let's implement this. We need to know where `nvcc` is. We can use `torch.utils.cpp_extension.CUDA_HOME` to find nvcc. We also need to ensure we link with `-lcudart`. `nvcc` usually handles that when compiling `.cu` files to `.so` with `-shared`. We need to handle the case where compilation might fail. Steps: 1. Get `CUDA_HOME` from torch (or environment variable `CUDA_HOME`/`CUDA_PATH`). 2. Find `nvcc`. 3. Create a temporary directory for the source and compiled output. 4. Write the `.cu` source. 5. Run `nvcc -shared -o kernel.so kernel.cu -O3 -use_fast_math` via `subprocess`. 6. Load the `.so` with `ctypes.CDLL`. 7. Call it. The CUDA source can be pure C++/CUDA without any torch headers. We just need pointer types. We also need to make sure the arguments passed from Python have contiguous memory layout and the correct format. The inputs (q, k, v, gate) are already float32 and in the shape (B, T, H, D), which is likely contiguous but not necessarily in the memory layout we assume. In the reference, the tensors are created with `torch.randn(batch_size, seq_len, num_heads, head_dim)` which is contiguous by default in PyTorch with shape (B, T, H, D). So `q.stride(0)=T*H*D`, `q.stride(1)=H*D`, `q.stride(2)=D`, `q.stride(3)=1`. Our kernel assuming this specific layout is safe as long as the tensors are contiguous in that shape. But we should verify we handle strides correctly or we could use pointer arithmetic according to actual strides. Using `q.data_ptr()` and computing offsets manually assumes contiguous. Let's ensure we call `.contiguous()` on inputs inside `forward()`. Actually, creating random tensors in the evaluation should be contiguous. But to be safe, we can call `.contiguous()` anyway. In terms of performance, `.contiguous()` is negligible if already contiguous. Let's design the kernel. The reference: ```python for t in range(seq_len): gate_t = gate[:, t].unsqueeze(-2) # (B, H, 1, D) kv_outer = k[:, t].unsqueeze(-1) * v[:, t].unsqueeze(-2) # (B, H, D, 1) * (B, H, 1, D) -> (B, H, D, D) state = gate_t * state + kv_outer # elementwise on last two dims? Wait: # state shape is (B, H, D, D) # gate_t shape is (B, H, 1, D) # gate_t * state: broadcasting (B,H,1,D) over (B,H,D,D) means gate_t is broadcast over the D dimension (third dim). # So state[b,h,i,j] = gate[b,h,0,j] * state[b,h,i,j] + k[b,h,t,i] * v[b,h,t,j] ``` Wait, let's re-express: `gate_t` is `gate[:, t].unsqueeze(-2)`. `gate[:, t]` has shape `(B, H, D)`. `unsqueeze(-2)` makes it `(B, H, 1, D)`. When multiplied by `state` of shape `(B, H, D, D)`, broadcasting applies: `(B, H, 1, D) * (B, H, D, D)` -> the result is `(B, H, D, D)` where the `1` is broadcast over the `D` dimension (the third dimension, which is the row index `i`). So each `state[b,h,i,j]` is multiplied by `gate[b,h,0,j]` (which is `gate[b,h,j]`). So the decay is per column `j` (output channel j), independent of row `i`. `kv_outer`: `k[:, t].unsqueeze(-1)` is `(B, H, D, 1)`, `v[:, t].unsqueeze(-2)` is `(B, H, 1, D)`. Their product is `(B, H, D, D)` where element `(i,j)` is `k[b,h,t,i] * v[b,h,t,j]`. Then `y_t = (q[:, t].unsqueeze(-1) * state).sum(dim=-2)`. `q[:, t].unsqueeze(-1)` is `(B, H, D, 1)`. Multiply by `state` `(B, H, D, D)` -> broadcasting `(D,1) * (D,D)` over the columns (last dim), resulting in `(B, H, D, D)` where element `(i,j)` is `q[b,h,t,i] * state[b,h,i,j]`. Sum over dim=-2 (the `i` dim, the second-to-last), yields `(B, H, D)`. So `y_t[b,h,j] = sum_i q[b,h,t,i] * state[b,h,i,j]`. So we need to compute for each `(b, h, j)`: - Maintain `state[b,h,i,j]` for all `i`. - For each time step `t`: - `state_i = gate[b,h,t,j] * state_i + k[b,h,t,i] * v[b,h,t,j]` for all `i`. - `y = sum_i q[b,h,t,i] * state_i`. This is exactly what my kernel was doing: each block computes for a single `(b, h, j)` pair, and iterates over time `t`. The kernel design seems correct. The only problem is the compilation environment. Let's pivot to the `ctypes` + `nvcc` compilation approach. Implementation of ModelNew class: ```python import torch import torch.nn as nn import os import subprocess import tempfile import ctypes CUDA_SOURCE = r''' // pure C++/CUDA, no torch headers #include <cuda_runtime.h> #include <device_launch_parameters.h> __device__ float warp_reduce_sum(float val) { #pragma unroll for (int offset = 16; offset > 0; offset >>= 1) { val += __shfl_down_sync(0xffffffff, val, offset); } return val; } __global__ void gated_delta_attention_kernel( const float* __restrict__ q, const float* __restrict__ k, const float* __restrict__ v, const float* __restrict__ gate, float* __restrict__ out, int batch_size, int seq_len, int num_heads, int head_dim) { int total_j = num_heads * head_dim; int b = blockIdx.x / total_j; int rem = blockIdx.x % total_j; int h = rem / head_dim; int j = rem % head_dim; int tid = threadIdx.x; int batch_stride = seq_len * num_heads * head_dim; int t_stride = num_heads * head_dim; int base = b * batch_stride + h * head_dim; extern __shared__ char smem[]; float* state = (float*)smem; float* sv_ptr = (float*)(smem + head_dim * sizeof(float)); float* sg_ptr = sv_ptr + 1; for (int ii = tid; ii < head_dim; ii += blockDim.x) { state[ii] = 0.0f; } __syncthreads(); for (int t = 0; t < seq_len; ++t) { int t_offset = t * t_stride; if (tid == 0) { *sv_ptr = v[base + t_offset + j]; *sg_ptr = gate[base + t_offset + j]; } __syncthreads(); float v_j = *sv_ptr; float g_j = *sg_ptr; float partial = 0.0f; for (int ii = tid; ii < head_dim; ii += blockDim.x) { float q_i = q[base + t_offset + ii]; float k_i = k[base + t_offset + ii]; float s = state[ii]; s = g_j * s + k_i * v_j; state[ii] = s; partial += q_i * s; } partial = warp_reduce_sum(partial); if (tid == 0) { out[base + t_offset + j] = partial; } __syncthreads(); } } extern "C" void gated_delta_attention_forward( const float* q, const float* k, const float* v, const float* gate, float* out, int batch_size, int seq_len, int num_heads, int head_dim) { int total_blocks = batch_size * num_heads * head_dim; int threads = 32; size_t shared_mem_size = head_dim * sizeof(float) + 2 * sizeof(float); gated_delta_attention_kernel<<<total_blocks, threads, shared_mem_size>>>( q, k, v, gate, out, batch_size, seq_len, num_heads, head_dim ); } ''' def compile_kernel(): cuda_home = os.environ.get('CUDA_HOME', os.environ.get('CUDA_PATH', None)) if cuda_home is None: try: import torch.utils.cpp_extension as ext cuda_home = ext.CUDA_HOME except Exception: pass if cuda_home is None: raise RuntimeError("CUDA_HOME not found") nvcc = os.path.join(cuda_home, 'bin', 'nvcc') if not os.path.exists(nvcc): raise RuntimeError(f"nvcc not found at {nvcc}") # create temporary directory temp_dir = tempfile.mkdtemp() cu_path = os.path.join(temp_dir, "kernel.cu") so_path = os.path.join(temp_dir, "kernel.so") with open(cu_path, "w") as f: f.write(CUDA_SOURCE) cmd = [ nvcc, "-shared", "-O3", "--use_fast_math", "-Xcompiler", "-fPIC", "-o", so_path, cu_path, ] result = subprocess.run(cmd, capture_output=True, text=True) if result.returncode != 0: raise RuntimeError(f"NVCC compilation failed:\n{result.stderr}") lib = ctypes.CDLL(so_path) lib.gated_delta_attention_forward.argtypes = [ ctypes.c_void_p, ctypes.c_void_p, ctypes.c_void_p, ctypes.c_void_p, ctypes.c_void_p, ctypes.c_int, ctypes.c_int, ctypes.c_int, ctypes.c_int, ] lib.gated_delta_attention_forward.restype = None return lib _kernel_lib = None def get_kernel_lib(): global _kernel_lib if _kernel_lib is None: _kernel_lib = compile_kernel() return _kernel_lib ``` Then ModelNew.forward: ```python def forward(self, q, k, v, gate): q = q.contiguous() k = k.contiguous() v = v.contiguous() gate = gate.contiguous() out = torch.empty_like(q) lib = get_kernel_lib() lib.gated_delta_attention_forward( ctypes.c_void_p(q.data_ptr()), ctypes.c_void_p(k.data_ptr()), ctypes.c_void_p(v.data_ptr()), ctypes.c_void_p(gate.data_ptr()), ctypes.c_void_p(out.data_ptr()), q.size(0), q.size(1), q.size(2), q.size(3) ) return out ``` Wait, PyTorch tensors could be on GPU. `ctypes.c_void_p(q.data_ptr())` works fine because data_ptr is just an integer address. The CUDA kernel launch inside the library will run on the current CUDA stream (the default stream). But there's a catch: if the kernel runs on the default stream and PyTorch uses the default stream, that's fine. However, when using `ctypes.CDLL`, the function is called synchronously on CPU. The kernel is launched asynchronously, but the function returns immediately. This is fine because PyTorch will synchronize later when the tensor is used. However, the `out` tensor needs to be kept alive until the kernel finishes. Since `out` is a local variable in `forward`, it stays alive. But there's a slight risk: the `ctypes.CDLL` call doesn't know about PyTorch tensors, so if `out` gets garbage collected before the kernel finishes, it could be bad. But `out` is returned, so it won't be GC'd. Also, the inputs are referenced by the Python caller. One caveat: the kernel uses `<<<...>>>` without any stream argument, so it goes to the default (legacy) stream. PyTorch's default stream is the legacy default stream. In modern PyTorch, the default stream is the legacy default stream. So that should be fine and synchronous with respect to other operations on the default stream. But there is a potential problem with the `lib` handle: if `lib` is a local variable and goes out of scope, the shared library might be unloaded by Python after the function returns? Actually, `ctypes.CDLL` holds a reference to the loaded library. If we store it globally (`_kernel_lib`), it's fine. The kernel launch itself is a pointer to the device function inside the library. Once launched, the kernel code is resident on the GPU, so even if the library is unloaded, the GPU kernel may crash. So we must ensure the library stays loaded. Using a global `_kernel_lib` is fine. Also, the temporary directory `temp_dir` might be cleaned up by the OS? No, `tempfile.mkdtemp()` creates a directory that persists. Good. But if `so_path` is in a temp directory and the process exits, it doesn't matter. If we want to be cleaner, we can store it somewhere persistent. In some environments, the `/tmp` directory may be cleaned by a cron job while the process is running, but it's unlikely during a short benchmark. One more thing: `nvcc` might not be in `CUDA_HOME/bin/nvcc`. Let's also try `which nvcc` by searching `PATH`. Let's refine the `compile_kernel` function. Also, we need to make sure the shapes and strides align. `q` is `(B, T, H, D)`, `stride=(T*H*D, H*D, D, 1)`. In the kernel, we access `base = b * batch_stride + h * head_dim` and then access elements at `t_offset = t * t_stride + i` or `j`. This corresponds exactly to a contiguous `(B, T, H, D)` tensor. We should enforce `.contiguous()` to ensure this. If we don't enforce `.contiguous()` on gate, and gate is non-contiguous (e.g., from `torch.sigmoid` on a contiguous tensor, which returns a contiguous tensor because `sigmoid` is an elementwise operation that preserves strides? Actually, `torch.sigmoid` preserves the input's memory layout. The input is `torch.randn(...)` which is contiguous. So the output is also contiguous. So it's fine. But `.contiguous()` is cheap. Let's fully write out the Python code. We need to be careful that `ctypes.CDLL` doesn't itself trigger any C++ runtime issues because the `.so` is compiled by `nvcc`. The `.so` compiled with `nvcc -shared` will contain C++ symbols for the CUDA runtime but the host-side function `gated_delta_attention_forward` is `extern "C"`, so it won't be name-mangled. The only C++ symbols in the `.so` might be generated by `nvcc` for device code, but `nvcc` handles its own runtime. Since we are not linking against libtorch or pybind11, there should be no `CXXABI` issue. The only host-side library we link against is `libcuda.so`, `libcudart.so`, and maybe C runtime. `nvcc` will use the system `g++` as the host compiler, and it might link against `libstdc++`. But if the `.so` doesn't export C++ symbols used by PyTorch, it doesn't matter if it depends on `libstdc++.so.6`, as long as `dlopen` resolves it. But we might still encounter the same `CXXABI` issue if `nvcc` compiles host code that requires the newer ABI and links dynamically to `libstdc++`. However, the code we wrote is very simple: `#include <cuda_runtime.h>` and `extern "C"`. `cuda_runtime.h` includes some C++ things? It might include some standard headers, but likely not newer C++ standard library features. `__restrict__` is a C99 keyword. The only issue could be that `nvcc` generates host code with C++ features. But `nvcc -shared` will link with `-lcudart`, and the host compiler might link with `-lstdc++` by default. If the resulting `.so` depends on `libstdc++.so.6` and the system version is too old, `ctypes.CDLL` could fail with the same error! Let's think: does `nvcc` link `-lstdc++` by default when compiling `.cu` to `.so`? Yes, `nvcc` uses `g++` as the host linker phase, and `g++` links `libstdc++` by default. If the `g++` version in the environment is new enough to compile the code, it should produce an object that is compatible with the `libstdc++` present in the environment IF `g++` uses that same `libstdc++`. But the problem was that the PyTorch extension compiled against the bundled `libstdc++` (which is newer), and the system one was older. If `nvcc` uses the system `g++`, it will link against the system `libstdc++`, which is older. If our code doesn't use any newer C++ features, the resulting `.so` should only require old `CXXABI` symbols, and it will work perfectly with the system `libstdc++`. That is the key. In the previous attempt with `load_inline`, the compiler used the headers from PyTorch, which required the new ABI. If we write a standalone `.cu` file without PyTorch headers, the compiler won't generate references to new ABI symbols. This is very likely to work. However, one caveat: `cuda_runtime.h` contains `__host__` and `__device__`, but host compilation of `.cu` files by `nvcc` uses the underlying host compiler. If the host compiler is GCC 11+, it might still generate references to newer symbols for things like `__restrict__` or `nullptr`, but those don't require `CXXABI_1.3.15`. `CXXABI_1.3.15` is needed for `std::string` and `std::ios_base` and some `type_info` features. So we should be safe. Let's write the code and test compilation. Another improvement for the kernel: Currently, we use 32 threads per block and `head_dim = 24`. We can set `blockDim.x = 32` but only threads 0..23 do work, threads 24..31 do nothing. That's okay. The `warp_reduce_sum` for these idle threads would add 0. So the partial sum from thread 0 is correct. But we can also optimize by loading q and k from global memory more efficiently. Observation: the `seq_len` recurrence is sequential, but we can process multiple time steps in parallel by using a parallel scan or by processing chunks. Since `gate_t` is a scalar per `(b,h,j)` and the recurrence is linear: `state_i(t) = gate(t) * state_i(t-1) + k_i(t) * v_j(t)`, we can unroll it over time. If we process a chunk of `T` steps in parallel: - Precompute prefix products of gate: `G(t) = prod_{tau=1..t} gate(tau)`. Actually, the recurrence is: `state_i(t) = (prod_{tau=1..t} gate_j(tau)) * state_i(0) + sum_{tau=1..t} (k_i(tau) * v_j(tau) * prod_{nu=tau+1..t} gate_j(nu))`. This is a first-order linear recurrence. It can be computed in parallel using associative scan (Blelloch scan) if we combine 2x2 matrices. For each time step `t` and each `i`, the transformation is: `[state_i(t)] = [gate_j(t), k_i(t)*v_j(t)] * [state_i(t-1)]` (affine transform) Actually, it's `state_i(t) = gate_j(t) * state_i(t-1) + k_i(t) * v_j(t)`. We can represent this as a linear transformation in homogeneous coordinates: `[state_i(t); 1] = [[gate_j(t), k_i(t)*v_j(t)]; [0, 1]] * [state_i(t-1); 1]` But we need a reduction for each `i` separately. The `gate_j(t)` is the same for all `i`, but the `k_i(t)*v_j(t)` term depends on `i`. For each `j`, we could do an associative scan across `t` for each `i`. But `head_dim = 24`, so for each `j` we have 24 independent scans of length 128. A scan per warp is possible but requires inter-warp communication. Alternatively, we can unroll the recurrence using warp-level matrix operations. Given the small size (B=2, H=4, D=24, T=128), the overhead of a scan might not be worth it compared to a simple loop, unless we can heavily parallelize across `t`. Wait, we can parallelize across `t` in chunks. For example, the output `y_t = sum_i q_i(t) * state_i(t)`. If we precompute the cumulative products and cumulative sums for each `j`, we can compute `state_i(t)` for all `t` in parallel if we know all inputs. This is what the associative scan does. Let's formalize: For a fixed `(b,h,j)`, and for each `i`: `state_i(t) = gate_j(t) * state_i(t-1) + k_i(t) * v_j(t)` with `state_i(-1) = 0` (initial state). This is a scalar first-order recurrence. `state_i(t) = sum_{tau=0..t} [ k_i(tau) * v_j(tau) * prod_{nu=tau+1..t} gate_j(nu) ]`. (where empty product over `nu > tau` is 1). So if we precompute prefix products of gate: `P(t) = prod_{nu=0..t} gate_j(nu)`. Then `prod_{nu=tau+1..t} gate_j(nu) = P(t) / P(tau)`. So `state_i(t) = P(t) * sum_{tau=0..t} [ k_i(tau) * v_j(tau) / P(tau) ]`. Then `y_j(t) = sum_i q_i(t) * state_i(t) = P(t) * sum_i q_i(t) * sum_{tau=0..t} [ k_i(tau) * v_j(tau) / P(tau) ]`. This can be rewritten as: For each `i`, define `x_i(tau) = k_i(tau) * v_j(tau) / P(tau)`. Then `S_i(t) = sum_{tau=0..t} x_i(tau)` (prefix sum). Then `state_i(t) = P(t) * S_i(t)`. Then `y_j(t) = P(t) * sum_i q_i(t) * S_i(t)`. Wait, is that correct? `state_i(t) = sum_{tau=0..t} k_i(tau)*v_j(tau) * product_{nu=tau+1..t} gate_j(nu)`. `P(t) = product_{nu=0..t} gate_j(nu)`. `P(tau) = product_{nu=0..tau} gate_j(nu)`. `product_{nu=tau+1..t} gate_j(nu) = P(t) / P(tau)`. So `state_i(t) = P(t) * sum_{tau=0..t} k_i(tau)*v_j(tau) / P(tau)`. Yes. So if we precompute `P(t)` and `1/P(tau)` for all `t`, we can compute `x_i(tau)` and then compute prefix sums over `tau` for each `i`. Then `y_j(t)` is a dot product of `q(t)` and `S(t)` scaled by `P(t)`. This allows us to parallelize across `t` much more efficiently. Step-by-step for each `(b, h, j)`: 1. Compute `gate_j(t)` for all `t`. Compute prefix product `P(t)` for all `t`. This is a scan over `t`. 2. Compute `inv_P(t) = 1 / P(t)` for all `t`. 3. For each `i` (0..D-1): - Compute `x_i(t) = k_i(t) * v_j(t) * inv_P(t)` for all `t`. - Compute prefix sum `S_i(t) = sum_{tau=0..t} x_i(tau)` for all `t`. 4. However, it allows massive parallelism: all `t` can be processed in parallel for steps 3-5. Step 1 is a scan over `t` of length 128. What if we process the recurrence in a way that allows parallelizing over `t` within a block? Consider the prefix product/prefix sum formulation. For each `(b,h,j)`: 1. Compute prefix product of gate over `t`. 2. Compute prefix sum of `k_i(t) * v_j(t) / P(t)` over `t` for each `i`. 3. Compute output. If we assign one block per `(b,h)`, and one warp per `j`? Or one block per `(b,h)` with all `j` handled in parallel. For `(b,h)`: - T = 128, D = 24. - Step 1: for each `j`, prefix product over `t`. This is 24 independent scans over `t`. A warp can do one scan using warp-shuffle or shared memory. A block of 24 warps can do 24 scans in parallel. - Step 2: for each `j` and each `i`, we need prefix sum of `x_i(t) = k_i(t) * v_j(t) / P(t)` over `t`. That's D * D = 576 independent scans over `t`! That's a lot of scans. However, we can reorganize: `y_j(t) = P(t) * sum_i q_i(t) * S_i(t)`, where `S_i(t) = prefix_sum_{tau<=t} (k_i(tau) * v_j(tau) / P(tau))`. We can think of `S_i(t)` as: for a fixed `i`, we need a vector of length T for each `j`. Or for a fixed `j`, we need D vectors of length T. For a fixed `j`, the quantity `v_j(tau) / P(tau)` is independent of `i`. So `x_i(tau) = k_i(tau) * (v_j(tau) / P(tau))`. For each `j`, if we precompute `w_j(tau) = v_j(tau) / P(tau)`, then for each `i` we need prefix sum of `k_i(tau) * w_j(tau)`. For fixed `j`, compute `prefix_sum_i(t) = prefix_sum_{tau} (k_i(tau) * w_j(tau))` for all `i`. This is like a matrix-vector product followed by a scan. Specifically, for fixed `j`, define matrix `A` of shape `(T, D)` where `A[tau, i] = k_i(tau) * w_j(tau)`. Then `S_i(t)` is the prefix sum of column `i` of `A`. Now `y_j(t) = P(t) * sum_i q_i(t) * S_i(t)`. We can compute `S_i(t)` for all `i` and `t` using a parallel algorithm. But the overhead of 24 scans of length 128 is small for a block. Not without breaking the recurrence. Unless... we process `t` in chunks and pipeline them. For example, chunk size `C=16`. For each `(b,h,j)`, break `t` into 8 chunks. For each chunk, compute the state at the beginning from the previous chunk. Since the recurrence is first-order linear, we can precompute the product of gate over the chunk to compress it. Specifically, define chunk `c` covering `t` from `c*C` to `(c+1)*C - 1`. For a fixed `(b,h,j)`, define: - `G_c = prod_{t=c*C}^{(c+1)*C - 1} gate_j(t)`. - `S_{c,i} = sum_{t=c*C}^{(c+1)*C - 1} [ k_i(t) * v_j(t) * prod_{u=t+1}^{(c+1)*C - 1} gate_j(u) ]`. Then the state update across chunks is: `state_{c+1} = G_c * state_c + S_c`. Where `state_c` is a vector of length D, and `S_c` is a vector of length D. And within a chunk, we can compute `S_c` in parallel because it's just a local prefix product over `C` steps. This is like a hierarchical scan. Then output `y_j(t)` within a chunk depends on the state at time `t`, which can be computed from `state_c` and the gate values within the chunk. This is getting complex. For B=2, H=4, T=128, D=24, it's probably overkill. The reference is a Python loop doing tiny PyTorch ops. Even a kernel with 192 warps will be much faster because it avoids 128 Python iterations and many kernel launches. The memory bandwidth is the same. Let's estimate reference runtime. The reference loop does 128 iterations. In each iteration: - `gate[:, t].unsqueeze(-2)`: small manipulation - `k[:, t].unsqueeze(-1) * v[:, t].unsqueeze(-2)`: outer product, results in (2,4,24,24) = 4608 elements. This launches a CUDA kernel. - `gate_t * state + kv_outer`: elementwise on (2,4,24,24). Another kernel launch. - `(q[:, t].unsqueeze(-1) * state).sum(dim=-2)`: broadcast multiply (2,4,24,24) + sum over dim=-2 (2,4,24). Another kernel launch. So per iteration, maybe 3 kernel launches, plus framework overhead. 128 * 3 = 384 kernel launches. Plus stacking at the end. Given the tiny tensor sizes, kernel launch overhead dominates. A single fused kernel that does everything in one launch should be orders of magnitude faster even if it underutilizes the GPU. So the 192-block design is likely enough to beat the reference by a large margin. Let's try it. One optimization: instead of each warp doing a `warp_reduce_sum`, which requires a loop of `__shfl_down_sync`, we can have each thread accumulate in register and then use warp shuffle to reduce. However, the state can be stored in registers if each thread is responsible for one `i` as we discussed. That eliminates shared memory entirely. In fact, each thread only needs to keep one float `state_i` in its register. It loads `q_i` and `k_i` each iteration. No need for shared memory. Wait, shared memory was only 104 bytes, so pressure is already zero. But removing shared memory means we don't need `__syncthreads`, avoiding some overhead. Revised kernel (no shared memory): ```cuda __global__ void gated_delta_attention_kernel( const float* q, const float* k, const float* v, const float* gate, float* out, int batch_size, int seq_len, int num_heads, int head_dim) { int total_j = num_heads * head_dim; int b = blockIdx.x / total_j; int rem = blockIdx.x % total_j; int h = rem / head_dim; int j = rem % head_dim; int tid = threadIdx.x; int batch_stride = seq_len * num_heads * head_dim; int t_stride = num_heads * head_dim; int base = b * batch_stride + h * head_dim; float state = 0.0f; float v_j, g_j; float partial; for (int t = 0; t < seq_len; ++t) { int t_offset = t * t_stride; int idx = base + t_offset; // Each thread loads its own q and k float q_i = (tid < head_dim) ? q[idx + tid] : 0.0f; float k_i = (tid < head_dim) ? k[idx + tid] : 0.0f; // Load v_j and gate_j - redundant but cached if (tid == 0) { v_j = v[idx + j]; g_j = gate[idx + j]; } // Broadcast within warp (no syncthreads needed for warp-level) v_j = __shfl_sync(0xffffffff, v_j, 0); g_j = __shfl_sync(0xffffffff, g_j, 0); if (tid < head_dim) { state = g_j * state + k_i * v_j; partial = q_i * state; } else { partial = 0.0f; } partial = warp_reduce_sum(partial); if (tid == 0) { out[idx + j] = partial; } } } ``` Wait, there is a subtle issue: `v_j` and `g_j` are loaded by thread 0 at each `t`. Since all threads in the block are in the same warp (block size = 32), we can use `__shfl_sync` to broadcast. This avoids shared memory. Nice! We need `warp_reduce_sum` to work across the full warp. We used `#pragma unroll` for `offset = 16, 8, 4, 2, 1`. That works for 32 threads. Even if `tid >= head_dim`, `partial` is 0, so the reduction is correct. One more thing: `out[idx + j]` is written by thread 0. Other threads don't participate in the write. Since it's a single float write, it's fine. offset > 0; offset >>= 1) { val += __shfl_down_sync(0xffffffff, val, offset); } return val; } __global__ void gated_delta_attention_kernel( const float* __restrict__ q, const float* __restrict__ k, const float* __restrict__ v, const float* __restrict__ gate, float* __restrict__ out, int batch_size, int seq_len, int num_heads, int head_dim) { int total_j = num_heads * head_dim; But there is a subtle CUDA stream issue: `gated_delta_attention_forward` launches a kernel on the default stream. If PyTorch is using a different stream, the kernel might not synchronize correctly. By default, PyTorch operations are on the default stream. The input tensors might be on a non-default stream if they were produced by an async operation on another stream. But in the reference model and typical evaluation, inputs are on the default stream. However, to be safe, we could call `torch.cuda.synchronize()` after the kernel. But that adds overhead. In `torch.utils.cpp_extension`, the generated pybind11 wrapper might handle streams? Actually, the auto-generated wrapper just calls the function; it doesn't set a stream. If the CUDA runtime functions are called directly, they use the NULL stream (the legacy default stream), which is synchronous with the per-thread default stream only if the context was created with certain flags. In modern PyTorch, the default stream is the per-thread default stream (PTDS). Kernels launched on the legacy default stream are NOT synchronous with PTDS, so we could have a race condition if PyTorch uses PTDS. Wait, what stream does PyTorch use? Since PyTorch 1.8+, the default stream is the per-thread default stream. The CUDA runtime's `<<<...>>>` without a stream argument uses the legacy default stream (stream 0). Synchronization between legacy default stream and PTDS is not automatic. So if we launch a kernel on stream 0, it might overlap with PyTorch operations on PTDS, causing correctness issues if the input tensors are being modified concurrently. To be safe, we should ensure our kernel runs on the same stream as PyTorch. How to get the current CUDA stream in raw C/CUDA without torch headers? We can use `cudaStream_t stream; cudaStreamGetCurrentStream(0, &stream);`? No, the CUDA runtime API doesn't have `cudaStreamGetCurrentStream`. It has `cudaStreamQuery`, but not "get current". Actually, there is no runtime API to get the current per-thread default stream. You can get the legacy default stream with `0`. Wait, there is `c10::cuda::getCurrentCUDAStream()` in libtorch. But we are avoiding libtorch. However, there is a CUDA driver API or runtime trick: `cudaStream_t stream = at::cuda::getCurrentCUDAStream(device)`? No, we don't have at:: headers. Can we pass the stream pointer from Python to the C function? Yes! PyTorch exposes the current CUDA stream via `torch.cuda.current_stream(device).cuda_stream`. This is an integer representing the `cudaStream_t` handle (which is actually a pointer). We can pass it to the C function as a `ctypes.c_void_p` and then use it in the kernel launch: `kernel<<<blocks, threads, smem, stream>>>(...)`. Yes! This is the proper way. `stream = torch.cuda.current_stream(q.device).cuda_stream` returns the integer handle. Pass it as `ctypes.c_void_p(stream)`. Then our host-side wrapper uses it. So in the `.cu` host function: ```c extern "C" void gated_delta_attention_forward(..., cudaStream_t stream) { kernel<<<..., stream>>>(...); } ``` Then in Python: ```python stream = torch.cuda.current_stream(q.device).cuda_stream _LIB.gated_delta_attention_forward(..., ctypes.c_void_p(stream)) ``` This ensures correct stream ordering. Let's update the CUDA source and Python wrapper accordingly. Also, we need to handle the case where `q.device` is CPU. The problem says `device=q.device`, but in the evaluation, inputs might be on GPU. We can assert `q.is_cuda`. Let's write the updated code.

reasoning
Bug: the state should be per channel index `i` (the head_dim dimension). Each output channel of the `head_dim x head_dim` state matrix decays independently. My current kernel computes `state` as a scalar per thread, but `state` should actually be a vector of `head_dim` elements because each `state[:, i]` under the original code is independent. Wait, let's re-examine the original code carefully: ```python state = torch.zeros(batch_size, num_heads, head_dim, head_dim) for t in range(seq_len): gate_t = gate[:, t].unsqueeze(-2) # shape (B, 1, H, D) kv_outer = k[:, t].unsqueeze(-1) * v[:, t].unsqueeze(-2) # shape (B, H, D, D) state = gate_t * state + kv_outer # elementwise: gate shape broadcasts y_t = (q[:, t].unsqueeze(-1) * state).sum(dim=-2) # q[:, t].unsqueeze(-1) is (B, H, D, 1); times state is (B, H, D, D); sum over dim=-2 yields (B, H, D) outputs.append(y_t) ``` Wait, let me carefully trace shapes: - `q[:, t]` shape: (B, H, D) - `q[:, t].unsqueeze(-1)` shape: (B, H, D, 1) - `state` shape: (B, H, D, D) - `q[:, t].unsqueeze(-1) * state` shape: (B, H, D, D) (broadcasting over the last dim) - `.sum(dim=-2)`: sum over dim=-2, which is the first of the two last dims (index -2). That corresponds to the dimension of size D that came from `q`'s head_dim. Result is (B, H, D). So `y_t[b, h, j] = sum_i q[b, t, h, i] * state[b, h, i, j]`. The state update per element: `state[b, h, i, j] = gate[b, t, h, j] * state[b, h, i, j] + k[b, t, h, i] * v[b, t, h, j]`. Ah! The gate does NOT depend on `i`, only on `j`. The `k` depends on `i` but not `j`. The `v` depends on `j` but not `i`. So for each `(b, h, j)`, the state across `i` evolves: `state_i = gate_j * state_i + k_i * v_j` And the output `y_j = sum_i q_i * state_i`. In my kernel, each block computes the whole output for a given `(b, h, j)`, and `tid` corresponds to `i`. So I should have an array `state[head_dim]` initialized to zero. But in my first incorrect attempt, each thread had a scalar `state`. That is actually correct conceptually because each thread handles one `i`. The thread computes: `state_i = g_j * state_i + k_i * v_j` `partial_i = q_i * state_i` Then we warp-reduce sum of partial_i to get `y_j`. Wait, this matches the formula! Why did correctness fail? Let's look at my kernel code more carefully. New shape: `(B, H, 1, D)`. `state` shape: `(B, H, D, D)`. `gate_t * state`: - dim 0: B matches. - dim 1: H matches. - dim 2: 1 broadcasts to D (the FIRST D of state, which corresponds to the `k` dimension). - dim 3: D matches (the SECOND D of state, which corresponds to the `v` dimension). New design: Process everything in one block per (b, h), with 24 threads. - Shared memory: `state[head_dim * head_dim]` for the full state matrix. Size = 24*24*4 = 2304 bytes. - Each thread handles one `j`. - For each `t`: - Thread `j` reads `v_j` and `g_j`. - All threads loop over `i` (broadcast via shared memory? no, each thread updates its column). - Actually, each thread `j` updates `state[i, j]` for all `i`. But each thread accesses its own column. With 24 threads and 32 banks, some threads might share banks depending on the access pattern. Bank = `(addr / 4) % 32`. For `j=0`: banks 0, 6, 12, 18, 24%32=24, 30, 4, ... (because 24 * i / 4 = 6i, bank = 6i % 32). For `j=1`: bank starts at 1. So thread 0 and thread 1 might conflict? Actually, shared memory banks are 4 bytes wide on modern GPUs. So `j=0` is bank 0, `j=1` is bank 1, etc. There is no conflict! So this design is perfectly bank-conflict-free for column-wise access. - Grid: `batch_size * num_heads` blocks. - Threads: `head_dim` = 24. (Use 32 for alignment, but 24 is fine). - Shared memory: `head_dim * head_dim * sizeof(float)` = 2304 bytes. - Per `t`: - Thread `j` loads `v_j` and `g_j` into registers. - `__syncthreads()` (actually not needed for register loads). - Thread `j` loops over `i`: - `s = state[i*head_dim + j]` - `s = g_j * s + k[b,t,h,i] * v_j` - `state[i*head_dim + j] = s` - `partial += q[b,t,h,i] * s` - After loop, write `out[b,t,h,j] = partial`. - `__syncthreads()` before next `t` (so all threads finish writing state). Wait, within a given `t`, thread `j` reads `k[b,t,h,i]` for all `i`. But `k[b,t,h,i]` is accessed by ALL threads simultaneously for the same `i`! That is, at step `i`, all threads read `k[...i]`. This is a broadcast and should be one memory transaction. Great! Similarly for `q`. This is highly efficient. But wait, thread `j` loops over `i` from 0 to `head_dim-1`. All threads do this simultaneously. So at each `i`, all threads read the same `k_i` and `q_i`, and update different columns of state. This is extremely efficient. There's almost no divergence. Let me write this kernel in the `ctypes` approach. I'll also add `cudaGetLastError` and `cudaDeviceSynchronize` for debugging. Actually, using 24 threads per block might be slightly less efficient than 32 for warp-level operations, but it's fine. I can also use 32 threads and have 8 threads idle. With 24 threads: - `tid` from 0 to 23 corresponds to `j`. - For `tid >= head_dim`, do nothing. - Need to pad shared memory to 32 for state? No, state is `head_dim * head_dim`. Each thread only accesses its own `j` column. Let's code this carefully. CUDA kernel: ```cpp __global__ void gated_delta_attention_kernel( const float* __restrict__ q, const float* __restrict__ k, const float* __restrict__ v, const float* __restrict__ gate, float* __restrict__ out, int seq_len, int num_heads, n int head_dim) { int b = blockIdx.x / num_heads; int h = blockIdx.x % num_heads; int j = threadIdx.x; if (j >= head_dim) return; int t_stride = num_heads * head_dim; int batch_stride = seq_len * t_stride; int base = b * batch_stride + h * head_dim; extern __shared__ float smem[]; float* state = smem; // initialize state to zero for (int i = 0; i < head_dim; ++i) { state[i * head_dim + j] = 0.0f; } // No syncthreads needed because each thread only accesses its own column j for (int t = 0; t < seq_len; ++t) { int t_offset = t * t_stride; int idx = base + t_offset; float v_j = v[idx + j]; float g_j = gate[idx + j]; float partial = 0.0f; for (int i = 0; i < head_dim; ++i) { float q_i = q[idx + i]; float k_i = k[idx + i]; float s = state[i * head_dim + j]; s = g_j * s + k_i * v_j; state[i * head_dim + j] = s; partial += q_i * s; } out[idx + j] = partial; __syncthreads(); // ensure all threads finish writing state before next t } } ``` Wait, is `__syncthreads()` inside a conditional block safe? No! `__syncthreads()` must be called by all threads in the block or none. If some threads return early with `if (j >= head_dim) return`, then `__syncthreads()` is undefined behavior because not all threads reach it. To fix this, I should launch exactly `head_dim` threads (24), or use a different approach. If I launch 24 threads, there is no early return. But CUDA blocks typically should have at least 32 threads for full warp utilization? Actually, 24 threads is fine, though the warp is not fully utilized. It avoids the `__syncthreads()` issue. But what if the tool/hardware requires a minimum of 32 threads? No, it should work with any number. Actually, I usually prefer 32 threads because warp reduction is simpler. But with this design, I don't need warp reduction! Each thread computes its own `partial` and writes its own `out[idx + j]`. No need for reduction! So with 24 threads per block, no warp reduction, no `__syncthreads` inside conditional, no shfl. Let's try this! But wait, is it safe to have no `__syncthreads` at all? Within a time step `t`: - Thread `j` reads `state[i, j]` and writes `state[i, j]` for all `i`. - No other thread accesses `state[i, j]` for the same `j`. - So within time step `t`, no race condition on `state`. - Between time step `t` and `t+1`: - Thread `j` finishes updating `state[*, j]` and writes `out[t, j]`. - But other threads might be on time step `t+1` while some are still on time step `t` (because of thread divergence). - However, there is no divergence inside the loop if all threads are active and run the same instructions. - Since all threads have the same amount of work (loop of `head_dim` iterations), they should be in sync. - BUT, memory latency can cause threads to get out of sync! In CUDA, threads in a warp execute in lockstep (SIMT), but memory operations are asynchronous. After a write to shared memory, the write might not be visible to other warps immediately. However, since we only have one warp (24 threads), all threads are in the SAME warp. In a single warp, threads are implicitly synchronized (except when they diverge, which they don't here). So `__syncthreads` is not strictly needed for a single warp! But to be safe, adding `__syncthreads` at the end of each `t` iteration is fine if all threads participate. But with 24 threads, do I need `__syncthreads`? Not for correctness within a warp. But if the compiler or hardware reorders shared memory writes, visibility to other warps in the same block might be delayed. Since there's only one warp, there's no "other warps". So it's safe. However, `nvcc` might optimize the shared memory accesses and reuse cached values? It shouldn't, because the write and read are separated by multiple instructions. But to be 100% safe, I can add `__threadfence_block()` or `__syncthreads()`. Since all threads are in one warp and execute together, `__syncthreads()` is safe. Actually, `__syncthreads()` in a single-warp block is safe but incurs a tiny overhead. Let's just include it for safety. But what if I launch 32 threads with `if (j >= head_dim) return;` at the beginning? Then `__syncthreads()` will deadlock because not all threads reach it. So I must either launch exactly `head_dim` threads, or avoid `__syncthreads`. If I avoid `__syncthreads`, then the early-return threads are fine. If I avoid `__syncthreads`: ```cpp for (int t = 0; t < seq_len; ++t) { if (j < head_dim) { // do everything } } ``` Since there's no syncthreads, the divergent threads can just idle. But within the `if (j < head_dim)` block, all 24 active threads are in the same warp. They execute in lockstep. The shared memory writes by the active threads are visible to each other within the warp without an explicit barrier? Yes, because a warp executes instructions in lockstep, and shared memory has a latency of a few cycles but by the time the next instruction reads it, it should be visible (assuming no read-after-write hazard within the same instruction... but s = state[...]; state[...] = s; is read then write, so no hazard). Actually, within a single instruction, there might be issues, but here the read and write are separate instructions. But between time steps, thread 1 might finish iteration `t` and start `t+1` while thread 2 is still finishing `t`? No, in a warp, if all threads follow the same control flow, they execute synchronously. The loop counter `t` is the same for all threads. They all start iteration `t` together and finish together (barring memory stalls, but the warp scheduler ensures they reconverge). So `__syncthreads` is unnecessary for a single warp. So I can safely launch 32 threads, with 24 active, and no `__syncthreads`. But the inactive threads (24-31) have `j >= head_dim` and do nothing. The active threads all execute in lockstep. Wait, but does the warp scheduler allow threads 0-23 to run while threads 24-31 are masked off? Yes. They still execute in lockstep for the active threads. No barrier needed. However, to be absolutely safe and avoid any subtle warp scheduling issues, I'll launch exactly `head_dim` threads (24 threads per block). This is less than a warp but is valid CUDA. But there's a nuance: if I launch 24 threads, the block has less than one warp. Does the hardware still schedule it as a warp with some inactive lanes? Yes. State initialization: ```cpp for (int i = 0; i < head_dim; ++i) { state[i * head_dim + j] = 0.0f; } ``` Thread `j` initializes column `j` of the state matrix. Correct. Loop over `t`: ```cpp float v_j = v[idx + j]; float g_j = gate[idx + j]; float partial = 0.0f; for (int i = 0; i < head_dim; ++i) { float q_i = q[idx + i]; float k_i = k[idx + i]; float s = state[i * head_dim + j]; s = g_j * s + k_i * v_j; state[i * head_dim + j] = s; partial += q_i * s; } out[idx + j] = partial; ``` This computes `partial = sum_i q_i * (g_j * state_{old} + k_i * v_j)`. And writes the new state back. This matches: `state_{i,j} = gate_j * state_{i,j} + k_i * v_j`. `y_j = sum_i q_i * state_{i,j}`. Wait! Actually, there is a known solution for this exact problem: ```python import os import sys import ctypes torch_lib = os.path.join(os.path.dirname(torch.__file__), 'lib') ctypes.CDLL(os.path.join(torch_lib, 'libstdc++.so.6'), mode=ctypes.RTLD_GLOBAL) ``` This loads the torch libstdc++ into the process. BUT, as I analyzed, because the system libstdc++ was loaded first (by python), new lookups for `libstdc++.so.6` by imported extensions still find the system one. The `RTLD_GLOBAL` only adds symbols to the global scope. It does NOT redirect existing dependencies. However, if the extension's `libstdc++.so.6` dependency is resolved by looking at the ALREADY LOADED `libstdc++.so.6` first (which is the system one), it will use it. But wait, with `RTLD_DEEPBIND`, the extension resolves its symbols from its own dependency tree first. If we set the rpath of the extension to the torch lib directory, then when resolving `libstdc++.so.6`, it will find the torch one FIRST (via rpath), and load it. Then `RTLD_DEEPBIND` ensures that symbols from THAT copy are used by the extension. This should work. So the combined approach: - `ctypes.CDLL(torch_libstdcxx, mode=ctypes.RTLD_GLOBAL)` to add new symbols to the global namespace (for other things). - Set `sys.setdlopenflags(os.RTLD_NOW | os.RTLD_DEEPBIND)` before `load_inline`. - Pass `extra_ldflags=['-Wl,-rpath,' + torch_lib]` so the extension's rpath points to the torch lib directory. But wait, `load_inline` might not pass `extra_ldflags` to the linker for the shared library correctly. reasoning
I switched the kernel strategy. Each block handles one (b, h), and threads within a block handle one output dimension j. We use shared memory to store state [head_dim x head_dim] as a matrix (col-major per j). Since each thread j updates one column of the state matrix, this is fully parallel across j. However, within the inner loop for the reduction, each thread needs to read state[i*head_dim + j]. There is no bank conflict if each thread reads a different column in the same row because each column belongs to a different bank (32 banks, head_dim=24). So that's okay. But I forgot __syncthreads in the loop, though because each thread only accesses its own j, no __syncthreads is needed. The updates are independent. Let's test correctness.

The first kernel (warp-based) assumed state is a local scalar per thread. The state update `state = g_j * state + k_i * v_j`. This is a scalar recurrence for each (i, j) pair. With `gate_i`: ```cpp for t: float q_i = (tid < head_dim) ? q[idx + tid] : 0.0f; float k_i = (tid < head_dim) ? k[idx + tid] : 0.0f; float g_i = (tid < head_dim) ? gate[idx + tid] : 0.0f; if (tid == 0) { v_j = v[idx + j]; } v_j = __shfl_sync(0xffffffff, v_j, 0); if (tid < head_dim) { state = g_i * state + k_i * v_j; partial = q_i * state; } partial = warp_reduce_sum(partial); if (tid == 0) out[idx + j] = partial; ``` This is a very natural kernel. `state` is local per thread (per i, since tid=i). `partial` is `q_i * state_i` for each thread. Warp reduce sums across i. Output is written by thread 0. This is clean. The only difference from my first kernel is `g_i` instead of `g_j`. For the shared-memory kernel: Each thread j loads v_j and loops over i, using gate_i. ```cpp for (int t = 0; t < seq_len; ++t) { int idx = base + t * t_stride; float v_j = v[idx + j]; float partial = 0.0f; for (int i = 0; i < head_dim; ++i) { float g_i = gate[idx + i]; float k_i = k[idx + i]; float q_i = q[idx + i]; float s = state[i * head_dim + j]; s = g_i * s + k_i * v_j; state[i * head_dim + j] = s; partial += q_i * s; } out[idx + j] = partial; } ``` This is also clean. Let me just implement the warp-based kernel with gate_i. ```cpp #include <cuda_runtime.h> __device__ float warp_reduce_sum(float val) { #pragma unroll for (int offset = 16; offset > 0; offset >>= 1) { val += __shfl_down_sync(0xffffffff, val, offset); } return val; } __global__ void gated_delta_attention_kernel( const float* __restrict__ q, const float* __restrict__ k, const float* __restrict__ v, const float* __restrict__ gate, float* __restrict__ out, int seq_len, int num_heads, int head_dim) { int b = blockIdx.x / (num_heads * head_dim); int rem = blockIdx.x % (num_heads * head_dim); int h = rem / head_dim; int j = rem % head_dim; int tid = threadIdx.x; int t_stride = num_heads * head_dim; int batch_stride = seq_len * t_stride; int base = b * batch_stride + h * head_dim; float state = 0.0f; float v_j = 0.0f; for (int t = 0; t < seq_len; ++t) { int t_offset = t * t_stride; int idx = base + t_offset; float q_i = (tid < head_dim) ? q[idx + tid] : 0.0f; float k_i = (tid < head_dim) ? k[idx + tid] : 0.0f; float g_i = (tid < head_dim) ? gate[idx + tid] : 0.0f; if (tid == 0) { v_j = v[idx + j]; } v_j = __shfl_sync(0xffffffff, v_j, 0); float partial = 0.0f; if (tid < head_dim) { state = g_i * state + k_i * v_j; partial = q_i * state; } partial = warp_reduce_sum(partial); if (tid == 0) { out[idx + j] = partial; } } } extern "C" void gated_delta_attention_forward( const float* q, const float* k, const float* v, const float* gate, float* out, int batch_size, int seq_len, int num_heads, int head_dim, cudaStream_t stream) { int total_blocks = batch_size * num_heads * head_dim; int threads = 32; gated_delta_attention_kernel<<<total_blocks, threads, 0, stream>>>( q, k, v, gate, out, seq_len, num_heads, head_dim ); } ``` Wait, the warp reduce sum sums across all 32 threads. For head_dim=24, threads 24-31 contribute 0. Good. But what about state for threads 24-31? It's always 0 because g_i=0 and k_i=0. This is fine. Actually, `state = g_i * state + k_i * v_j`. For tid>=24, this is state = 0. This propagates correctly. One more minor thing: `__shfl_sync` for v_j. If tid==0 loads v_j, other threads get it. In the first iteration, v_j is 0 for all threads until tid==0 loads it. Then it is broadcast. Good. I think this is correct. Let me code it up. For batch_size=2, num_heads=4, head_dim=24: total_blocks = 2 * 4 * 24 = 192. blockIdx.x ranges from 0 to 191. b = blockIdx.x / 96. rem = blockIdx.x % 96. h = rem / 24. j = rem % 24. This is correct. One more issue: `v_j` is declared outside the loop and initialized to 0. `v_j = __shfl_sync(0xffffffff, v_j, 0)`. In the first iteration, before `if (tid == 0) { v_j = v[idx + j]; }`, v_j is 0 for all threads. Then tid==0 loads the real value. Then `__shfl_sync` broadcasts it. Correct.
